</table>
<p>The <b>Snake River</b> is a major river of the greater <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Pacific Northwest</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. At 1,078 miles (1,735 km) long, it is the largest <a href="/wiki/Tributary" title="Tributary">tributary</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_River" title="Columbia River">Columbia River</a>, the largest North American river that empties into the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGSrivers_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGSrivers-9">[9]</a></sup> Rising in western <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a>, the river flows through the <a href="/wiki/Snake_River_Plain" title="Snake River Plain">Snake River Plain</a> of southern <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>, then through the rugged <a href="/wiki/Hells_Canyon" title="Hells Canyon">Hells Canyon</a> area via northeastern <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a> and the rolling <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palouse_Hills" title="Palouse Hills">Palouse Hills</a>, to reach its mouth near the <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a> <a href="/wiki/Tri-Cities,_Washington" title="Tri-Cities, Washington">Tri-Cities</a> area, where it enters the Columbia. Its <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a> encompasses parts of six <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. states</a>, and its average <a href="/wiki/Discharge_(hydrology)" title="Discharge (hydrology)">discharge</a> is over 54,000 cubic feet per second (1,500 m<sup>3</sup>/s).</p>
<p>Rugged mountains divided by rolling plains characterize the physiographically diverse watershed of the Snake River. The Snake River Plain was created by a volcanic <a href="/wiki/Hotspot_(geology)" title="Hotspot (geology)">hotspot</a> which now lies underneath <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">Yellowstone National Park</a>, where the headwaters of the Snake River arise. Gigantic glacial-retreat flooding episodes that occurred during the previous <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">Ice Age</a> carved out many topographical features, including various canyons and ridges along the middle and lower Snake River. Two of these catastrophic flooding events significantly affected the river and its surrounds.</p>
<p>More than 11,000 years ago, prehistoric <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native Americans</a> lived along the Snake. <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">Salmon</a> from the Pacific Ocean spawned in the millions in the river. These fish were central to the lives of the people along the Snake below <a href="/wiki/Shoshone_Falls" title="Shoshone Falls">Shoshone Falls</a>. By the time <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Clark_Expedition" title="Lewis and Clark Expedition">Lewis and Clark</a> crossed the Rockies and sighted the valley of a Snake tributary, the <a href="/wiki/Nez_Perce_people" title="Nez Perce people">Nez Perce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shoshone" title="Shoshone">Shoshone</a> were the most powerful peoples in the region. Some tribes adopted use of horses after contact with Europeans, which reshaped their hunting and cultures for the next few hundred years before outside settlement. Later explorers and fur trappers further changed and used the resources of the Snake River basin. At one point, a hand sign made by the Shoshones representing fish was misinterpreted to represent a snake, giving the Snake River its name.</p>
<p>By the middle 19th century, the <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Trail" title="Oregon Trail">Oregon Trail</a>, a pioneer trail of which a major portion followed the Snake River, had been established by aspiring settlers and traders. <a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">Steamboats</a> and railroads moved agricultural products and minerals along the river throughout the 19th and early 20th centuries. The powerful, steep flow of the Snake River has been used since the 1890s to generate <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a>, enhance navigation, and provide irrigation water from fifteen major dams built on the lower river, transforming it into a series of <a href="/wiki/Reservoir" title="Reservoir">reservoirs</a>. Several of these have been proposed for removal in order to restore some of the river's once-tremendous salmon runs.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Course">Course</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Course">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Snake_River_with_Teton_Range_in_background.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="1000" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Snake_River_with_Teton_Range_in_background.jpg/200px-Snake_River_with_Teton_Range_in_background.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Snake_River_with_Teton_Range_in_background.jpg/300px-Snake_River_with_Teton_Range_in_background.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Snake_River_with_Teton_Range_in_background.jpg/400px-Snake_River_with_Teton_Range_in_background.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Snake_River_with_Teton_Range_in_background.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Snake River exits <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Lake" title="Jackson Lake">Jackson Lake</a> and winds southwards through <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Hole" title="Jackson Hole">Jackson Hole</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Formed by the confluence of three tiny headstreams on the southwest flank of Two Oceans Plateau in western <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">Yellowstone National Park</a>, the Snake starts out as a small river flowing west and south into <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Lake" title="Jackson Lake">Jackson Lake</a>. Its first 50-mile (80 km) run through the valley of <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Hole" title="Jackson Hole">Jackson Hole</a>, which cuts between the <a href="/wiki/Teton_Range" title="Teton Range">Teton Range</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_Divide" title="Continental Divide">Continental Divide</a>. The Snake takes a large bend northwest through <a href="/wiki/Snake_River_Canyon_(Wyoming)" title="Snake River Canyon (Wyoming)">Snake River Canyon</a>, cutting through the <a href="/wiki/Snake_River_Range" title="Snake River Range">Snake River Range</a> and into eastern <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>, receiving first the <a href="/wiki/Hoback_River" title="Hoback River">Hoback</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greys_River" title="Greys River">Greys</a> rivers before entering Palisades Reservoir. There it is also met by the <a href="/wiki/Salt_River_(Wyoming)" title="Salt River (Wyoming)">Salt River</a> at the mouth of <a href="/wiki/Star_Valley" title="Star Valley">Star Valley</a>. After passing through <a href="/wiki/Palisades_Dam" title="Palisades Dam">Palisades Dam</a>, the Snake River flows through the <a href="/wiki/Snake_River_Plain" title="Snake River Plain">Snake River Plain</a>, a vast <a href="/wiki/Physiographic_province" title="Physiographic province">physiographic province</a> extending through southern Idaho across the massif of the Rocky Mountains and underlain by the <a href="/wiki/Snake_River_Aquifer" title="Snake River Aquifer">Snake River Aquifer</a>, one of the most productive aquifers in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TributaryBasins_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-TributaryBasins-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uppersnakeprovince_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-uppersnakeprovince-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-groundwater_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-groundwater-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-topoquest_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-topoquest-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACMEmapper_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-ACMEmapper-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Southwest of the city of <a href="/wiki/Rexburg,_Idaho" title="Rexburg, Idaho">Rexburg</a>, the Snake receives from the right the <a href="/wiki/Henrys_Fork_(Snake_River)" title="Henrys Fork (Snake River)">Henrys Fork</a>, sometimes called the North Fork of the Snake River. The confluence with the Henrys Fork takes the river southwards through downtown <a href="/wiki/Idaho_Falls,_Idaho" title="Idaho Falls, Idaho">Idaho Falls</a>, rounding the <a href="/wiki/Fort_Hall_Indian_Reservation" title="Fort Hall Indian Reservation">Fort Hall Indian Reservation</a> and into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Falls_Reservoir" title="American Falls Reservoir">American Falls Reservoir</a>, receiving the <a href="/wiki/Portneuf_River_(Idaho)" title="Portneuf River (Idaho)">Portneuf River</a>. The Portneuf River Valley is an overflow channel that in the <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a> carried floodwaters from pluvial <a href="/wiki/Lake_Bonneville" title="Lake Bonneville">Lake Bonneville</a> into the Snake River Plain, carving out many topographic features and significantly altering the Snake River landscape. The Snake River resumes its journey westwards, then enters the <a href="/wiki/Snake_River_Canyon_(Idaho)" title="Snake River Canyon (Idaho)">Snake River Canyon of Idaho</a>, where it drops over <a href="/wiki/Shoshone_Falls" title="Shoshone Falls">Shoshone Falls</a>, a waterfall that marks the historical upriver limit of migrating <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TributaryBasins_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-TributaryBasins-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and passing under the <a href="/wiki/Perrine_Bridge" title="Perrine Bridge">Perrine Bridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uppersnakeprovince_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-uppersnakeprovince-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Close to <a href="/wiki/Twin_Falls,_Idaho" title="Twin Falls, Idaho">Twin Falls</a>, the Snake approaches the southernmost point in its entire course, after which it starts to flow generally northwest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TributaryBasins_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-TributaryBasins-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uppersnakeprovince_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-uppersnakeprovince-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-topoquest_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-topoquest-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACMEmapper_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-ACMEmapper-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lewiston_and_Clarkston.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1403" data-file-width="2048" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Lewiston_and_Clarkston.jpg/220px-Lewiston_and_Clarkston.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Lewiston_and_Clarkston.jpg/330px-Lewiston_and_Clarkston.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Lewiston_and_Clarkston.jpg/440px-Lewiston_and_Clarkston.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lewiston_and_Clarkston.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Clearwater_River_(Idaho)" title="Clearwater River (Idaho)">Clearwater River</a> (left) joins the Snake River (center) at <a href="/wiki/Lewiston,_Idaho" title="Lewiston, Idaho">Lewiston</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shortly after it passes within 30 miles (48 km) of the Idaho state capital of <a href="/wiki/Boise,_Idaho" title="Boise, Idaho">Boise</a>, the river surges past the state border into <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, close to where it meets the <a href="/wiki/Owyhee_River" title="Owyhee River">Owyhee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boise_River" title="Boise River">Boise</a> and <a href="/wiki/Payette_River" title="Payette River">Payette</a> rivers. The Snake River begins to define the roughly 200-mile-long (320 km) Idaho–Oregon state border, which follows the river into <a href="/wiki/Hells_Canyon" title="Hells Canyon">Hells Canyon</a>, a steep and spectacular gorge that cuts through the <a href="/wiki/Salmon_River_Mountains" title="Salmon River Mountains">Salmon River Mountains</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_Mountains_(Oregon)" title="Blue Mountains (Oregon)">Blue Mountains</a> of Idaho and Oregon. Hells Canyon is one of the most rugged and treacherous portions of the course of the Snake River, which <a href="/wiki/American_pioneer" title="American pioneer">pioneers</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Trail" title="Oregon Trail">Oregon Trail</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">steamboat</a> operators in the 19th century had great difficulty negotiating. There were hundreds of <a href="/wiki/Rapids" title="Rapids">rapids</a> in Hells Canyon, some of which have been stilled by the three dams of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hells_Canyon_Hydroelectric_Project" title="Hells Canyon Hydroelectric Project">Hells Canyon Hydroelectric Project</a>: <a href="/wiki/Hells_Canyon_Dam" title="Hells Canyon Dam">Hells Canyon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxbow_Dam" title="Oxbow Dam">Oxbow</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Brownlee_Dam" title="Brownlee Dam">Brownlee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TributaryBasins_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-TributaryBasins-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-topoquest_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-topoquest-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACMEmapper_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-ACMEmapper-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HellsCanyonDamProjects_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-HellsCanyonDamProjects-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Salmon_River_(Idaho)" title="Salmon River (Idaho)">Salmon River</a>, the largest tributary of the Snake River, meets it in one of the most remote areas of its entire course, nearly at the halfway point in Hells Canyon. From there, the Snake crosses into <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a> and Idaho, receiving the <a href="/wiki/Grande_Ronde_River" title="Grande Ronde River">Grande Ronde River</a> from the west before receiving the <a href="/wiki/Clearwater_River_(Idaho)" title="Clearwater River (Idaho)">Clearwater River</a> at <a href="/wiki/Lewiston,_Idaho" title="Lewiston, Idaho">Lewiston</a>, the uppermost major city on the navigable stretch of the Snake. As the Snake leaves Hells Canyon and spreads into the low-lying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palouse_Hills" title="Palouse Hills">Palouse Hills</a> of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Washington" title="Eastern Washington">eastern Washington</a>, the Lower Snake River Project's four dams have transformed the Snake River into a series of reservoirs. The confluence of the Snake and Columbia rivers has been submerged in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Wallula" title="Lake Wallula">Lake Wallula</a>, the reservoir of <a href="/wiki/McNary_Dam" title="McNary Dam">McNary Dam</a>. The Columbia River flows about 325 miles (523 km) further west to the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>, cutting through the <a href="/wiki/Cascade_Range" title="Cascade Range">Cascade Range</a> by way of the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_River_Gorge" title="Columbia River Gorge">Columbia River Gorge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TributaryBasins_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-TributaryBasins-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-topoquest_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-topoquest-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACMEmapper_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-ACMEmapper-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As recently as 165 million years ago, most of western North America was still part of the Pacific Ocean. The nearly complete subduction of the <a href="/wiki/Farallon_Plate" title="Farallon Plate">Farallon Plate</a> underneath the westward-moving <a href="/wiki/North_American_Plate" title="North American Plate">North American Plate</a> created the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a>, which were pushed up by rising <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> trapped between the sinking Farallon plate and the North American plate. As the North American Plate moved westwards over a stationary <a href="/wiki/Hotspot_(geology)" title="Hotspot (geology)">hotspot</a> beneath the crust, a series of tremendous lava flows and volcanic eruptions carved out the Snake River Plain beginning about 12 million years ago, west of the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Divide_of_the_Americas" title="Continental Divide of the Americas">Continental Divide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SRPGeology_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-SRPGeology-18">[18]</a></sup> Even larger lava flows of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbia_River_basalts" title="Columbia River basalts">Columbia River basalts</a> issued over eastern Washington, forming the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Plateau" title="Columbia Plateau">Columbia Plateau</a> southeast of the Columbia River and the Palouse Hills in the lower Snake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Separate volcanic activity formed the northwestern portion of the plain, an area far from the path of the hotspot which now lies beneath Yellowstone National Park.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SRPGeology_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-SRPGeology-18">[18]</a></sup> At this point, the Snake River watershed was beginning to take shape.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HotspotsSRP_update2013.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="730" data-file-width="1063" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/HotspotsSRP_update2013.JPG/220px-HotspotsSRP_update2013.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/HotspotsSRP_update2013.JPG/330px-HotspotsSRP_update2013.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/HotspotsSRP_update2013.JPG/440px-HotspotsSRP_update2013.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HotspotsSRP_update2013.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Locations of the Yellowstone hotspot as it passed through the <a href="/wiki/Snake_River_Plain" title="Snake River Plain">Snake River Plain</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Snake River Plain and the gap between the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Nevada_(U.S.)" title="Sierra Nevada (U.S.)">Sierra Nevada</a> and Cascade Range formed a "moisture channel," running as far inland as the headwaters of the Snake River. Rainclouds from the Pacific Ocean blown into the moisture channel travel eastwards over 1,000 miles (1,600 km). When the Teton Range uplifted about 9 million years ago along a <a href="/wiki/Detachment_fault" title="Detachment fault">detachment fault</a> running north–south through the central Rockies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> rainclouds began to encounter a barrier at the eastern end of the channel, engorging the headwaters of the Snake River with frequent rainfall. These rains fed the Snake River, helping it to cut through the Tetons, forming the Snake River Canyon of Wyoming. About 6 million years ago, the Salmon River Mountains and Blue Mountains at the far end of the plain began to rise, and as the river cut through the rising mountains, the ancestral Hells Canyon was formed. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Idaho" title="Lake Idaho">Lake Idaho</a>, formed during the <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a>, covered a large portion of the Snake River Plain between Twin Falls and Hells Canyon, and its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lava_dam" title="Lava dam">lava dam</a> was finally breached about 2 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shoshone_falls.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1117" data-file-width="1500" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Shoshone_falls.jpg/220px-Shoshone_falls.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Shoshone_falls.jpg/330px-Shoshone_falls.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Shoshone_falls.jpg/440px-Shoshone_falls.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shoshone_falls.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Shoshone_Falls" title="Shoshone Falls">Shoshone Falls</a> near <a href="/wiki/Twin_Falls,_Idaho" title="Twin Falls, Idaho">Twin Falls, Idaho</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Lava flowing from Cedar Butte in present southeast Idaho blocked the Snake River at Eagle Rock, about 42,000 years ago, near the present-day site of <a href="/wiki/American_Falls_Dam" title="American Falls Dam">American Falls Dam</a>. A 40-mile-long (64 km) lake, known as American Falls Lake, formed behind the barrier. The lake was stable and survived for nearly 30,000 years. About 14,500 years ago, <a href="/wiki/Pluvial" title="Pluvial">pluvial</a> Lake Bonneville in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Salt_Lake" title="Great Salt Lake">Great Salt Lake</a> area, formed in the last glacial period, spilled catastrophically down the Portneuf River into the Snake in an event known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bonneville_Flood" title="Bonneville Flood">Bonneville Flood</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> This was one of the first in a series of catastrophic flooding events in the Northwest known as the Ice Age Floods.</p>
<p>The deluge caused American Falls Lake to breach its natural lava dam, which was rapidly eroded with only the 50-foot-high (15 m) American Falls left in the end. The flood waters of Lake Bonneville, approximately twenty times the flow of the Columbia River or 5,300,000 cubic feet per second (150,000 m<sup>3</sup>/s), swept down the Snake River, leaving debris and sediment deposits across southern Idaho. For miles on either side of the Snake, flood waters stripped away soils and scoured the underlying basalt bedrock, in the process creating Shoshone Falls, Twin Falls, Crane Falls, and Swan Falls, while cutting and deepening gorges and canyons along the way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNGeology_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNGeology-23">[23]</a></sup> The Bonneville flood waters continued through Hells Canyon. The flood widened Hells Canyon but did not deepen it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HellsCanyonGeology_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-HellsCanyonGeology-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HAFO_snake_river.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="180" data-file-width="375" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/HAFO_snake_river.jpg/220px-HAFO_snake_river.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/HAFO_snake_river.jpg/330px-HAFO_snake_river.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/69/HAFO_snake_river.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HAFO_snake_river.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The extraordinarily flat and expansive Snake River Plain was the result of the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Bonneville" title="Lake Bonneville">Lake Bonneville</a> floods and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yellowstone_Hotspot" title="Yellowstone Hotspot">Yellowstone Hotspot</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As the Bonneville Floods rushed down the Snake River, the <a href="/wiki/Missoula_Floods" title="Missoula Floods">Missoula Floods</a> occurred in the same period, but farther north. The Missoula Floods, which took place more than 40 times in the time span from 15,000 to 13,000 years ago, were caused by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glacial_Lake_Missoula" title="Glacial Lake Missoula">Glacial Lake Missoula</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clark_Fork_(river)" title="Clark Fork (river)">Clark Fork</a> repeatedly being impounded by ice dams then breaking through, with the lake's water rushing over much of eastern Washington in massive surges far larger than the Lake Bonneville Flood. These floods pooled behind the Cascade Range into enormous lakes and spilled over the northern <a href="/wiki/Drainage_divide" title="Drainage divide">drainage divide</a> of the Snake River watershed, carving deep canyons through the Palouse Hills. The <a href="/wiki/Palouse_River" title="Palouse River">Palouse River</a> canyon was the largest of the many gorges cut through the Palouse Hills, and could not have become as large as it now is if it were not for the Missoula Floods. The Lake Bonneville Floods and the Missoula Floods helped widen and deepen the Columbia River Gorge, a giant <a href="/wiki/Water_gap" title="Water gap">water gap</a> which allows water from the Columbia and Snake rivers to take a direct route through the Cascade Range to the Pacific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HellsCanyonGeology_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-HellsCanyonGeology-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>The massive amounts of sediment deposited by the Lake Bonneville Floods in the Snake River Plain also had a lasting effect on most of the middle Snake River. The high <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_conductivity" title="Hydraulic conductivity">hydraulic conductivity</a> of the mostly-<a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalt</a> rocks in the plain led to the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Snake_River_Aquifer" title="Snake River Aquifer">Snake River Aquifer</a>, one of the most productive aquifers in North America. Many rivers and streams flowing from the north side of the plain sink into the aquifer instead of flowing into the Snake River, a group of watersheds called the <a href="/wiki/Lost_streams_of_Idaho" title="Lost streams of Idaho">lost streams of Idaho</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The aquifer filled to hold nearly 100,000,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acre_foot" title="Acre foot">acre feet</a> (120 km<sup>3</sup>) of water, underlying about 10,000 square miles (26,000 km<sup>2</sup>) in a plume 1,300 feet (400 m) thick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In places, water exits from rivers at rates of nearly 600 cubic feet per second (17 m<sup>3</sup>/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNGeology_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-PNGeology-23">[23]</a></sup> Much of the water lost by the Snake River as it transects the plain issues back into the river at its western end, by way of many <a href="/wiki/Artesian_aquifer" title="Artesian aquifer">artesian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spring_(hydrosphere)" title="Spring (hydrosphere)">springs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-groundwater_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-groundwater-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGSnawqa_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGSnawqa-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-springs_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-springs-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Watershed">Watershed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Watershed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbia_River_Basin" title="Columbia River Basin">Columbia River Basin</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Snake_River_in_Washington.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Snake_River_in_Washington.jpg/220px-Snake_River_in_Washington.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Snake_River_in_Washington.jpg/330px-Snake_River_in_Washington.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Snake_River_in_Washington.jpg/440px-Snake_River_in_Washington.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Snake_River_in_Washington.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Snake River flows through an arid gorge nearing its mouth in Washington</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Snake River is the thirteenth longest river in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGSrivers_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGSrivers-9">[9]</a></sup> Its watershed is the 10th largest among North American rivers, and covers almost 108,000 square miles (280,000 km<sup>2</sup>) in portions of six U.S. states: Wyoming, Idaho, <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>, Oregon, and <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>, with the largest portion in Idaho. Most of the Snake River watershed lies between the Rocky Mountains on the east and the Columbia Plateau on the northwest. The largest tributary of the Columbia River, the Snake River watershed makes up about 41% of the entire Columbia River Basin. Its average discharge at the mouth constitutes 31% of the Columbia's flow at that point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Above the confluence, the Snake is slightly longer than the Columbia—1,078 miles (1,735 km)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHD_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-NHD-5">[5]</a></sup> compared to 928 miles (1,493 km)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>—and its drainage basin is slightly larger—4% bigger than the upstream Columbia River watershed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>The mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-arid" title="Semi-arid">semi-arid</a>, even <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">desert</a> climate of the Snake River watershed on average, receives less than 12 inches (300 mm) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a> per year. However, precipitation in the Snake River watershed varies widely. At Twin Falls, in the center of the Snake River Plain, the climate is nearly desert, with an annual rainfall of just 9.24 inches (235 mm), although the average snowfall is 13.1 inches (330 mm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> This desert climate occupies the majority of the basin of the Snake River, so although it is longer than the Columbia River above the Tri-Cities, its discharge is on average significantly less. However, in the high Rockies of Wyoming, in the upper Jackson Hole area, the average precipitation is over 30 inches (760 mm), and snowfall averages 252 inches (6,400 mm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Most of the Snake River basin consists of wide, arid plains and rolling hills, bordered by high mountains. In the upper parts of the watershed, however, the river flows through an area with a distinct <a href="/wiki/Alpine_climate" title="Alpine climate">alpine climate</a>. There are also stretches where the river and its tributaries have incised themselves into tight gorges. The Snake River watershed includes parts of Yellowstone National Park, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Teton_National_Park" title="Grand Teton National Park">Grand Teton National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hells_Canyon_National_Recreation_Area" title="Hells Canyon National Recreation Area">Hells Canyon National Recreation Area</a>, and many other national and state parks.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbiarivermapsnakeriverhighlighted.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1500" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Columbiarivermapsnakeriverhighlighted.png/220px-Columbiarivermapsnakeriverhighlighted.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Columbiarivermapsnakeriverhighlighted.png/330px-Columbiarivermapsnakeriverhighlighted.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Columbiarivermapsnakeriverhighlighted.png/440px-Columbiarivermapsnakeriverhighlighted.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columbiarivermapsnakeriverhighlighted.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbia_River_Basin" title="Columbia River Basin">Columbia River Basin</a> with the Snake River highlighted in yellow and the Columbia River in blue</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Much of the area along the river, within a few miles of its banks, is irrigated farmland, especially in its middle and lower course. Irrigation dams include American Falls Dam, <a href="/wiki/Minidoka_Dam" title="Minidoka Dam">Minidoka Dam</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C.J._Strike_Dam" title="C.J. Strike Dam">C.J. Strike Dam</a>. Aside from water from the river, water is also pulled from the Snake River Aquifer for irrigation. Major cities along the river include <a href="/wiki/Jackson,_Wyoming" title="Jackson, Wyoming">Jackson</a> in Wyoming, Twin Falls, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Idaho_Falls" title="Idaho Falls">Idaho Falls</a>, Boise, and Lewiston in Idaho, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tri-Cities_(Washington)" title="Tri-Cities (Washington)">Tri-Cities</a> in Washington (<a href="/wiki/Kennewick,_Washington" title="Kennewick, Washington">Kennewick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pasco,_Washington" title="Pasco, Washington">Pasco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richland,_Washington" title="Richland, Washington">Richland</a>). There are fifteen dams in total along the Snake River, which aside from irrigation, also produce electricity, maintain a navigation channel along part of the river's route, and provide flood control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IdahoPowerDams_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-IdahoPowerDams-38">[38]</a></sup> However, fish passage is limited to the stretch below Hells Canyon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Snake River watershed is bounded by several other major North American watersheds, which drain both to the Atlantic or the Pacific, or into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endorheic" title="Endorheic">endorheic</a> basins. On the southwest side a divide separates the Snake watershed from Oregon's <a href="/wiki/Harney_Basin" title="Harney Basin">Harney Basin</a>, which is endorheic. On the south, the Snake watershed borders that of the <a href="/wiki/Humboldt_River" title="Humboldt River">Humboldt River</a> in Nevada, and the watershed of the Great Salt Lake (the <a href="/wiki/Bear_River_(Utah)" title="Bear River (Utah)">Bear</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan_River_(Utah)" title="Jordan River (Utah)">Jordan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weber_River" title="Weber River">Weber</a> rivers) on the south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NorthAmericaWatersheds_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-NorthAmericaWatersheds-40">[40]</a></sup> The Snake River also shares a boundary with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_River_(Utah)" title="Green River (Utah)">Green River</a> to the southeast; the Green River drains parts of Wyoming and Utah and is the largest tributary of the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_River" title="Colorado River">Colorado River</a>. On the western extremity for a short stretch the Continental Divide separates the Snake watershed from the <a href="/wiki/Bighorn_River" title="Bighorn River">Bighorn River</a>, a tributary of the <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_River" title="Yellowstone River">Yellowstone River</a>, which the Snake begins near. On the north the Snake River watershed is bounded by the <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_River" title="Jefferson River">Red Rock River</a>, a tributary of the <a href="/wiki/Beaverhead_River" title="Beaverhead River">Beaverhead River</a>, which flows into the Jefferson River and into the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_River" title="Missouri River">Missouri River</a>, part of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> drainage basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NorthAmericaWatersheds_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-NorthAmericaWatersheds-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>The rest of the Snake River watershed borders on several other major Columbia River tributaries - mostly the <a href="/wiki/Spokane_River" title="Spokane River">Spokane River</a> to the north, but also Clark Fork in <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a> to the northeast and the <a href="/wiki/John_Day_River" title="John Day River">John Day River</a> to the west. Of these, the Clark Fork (via the <a href="/wiki/Pend_Oreille_River" title="Pend Oreille River">Pend Oreille River</a>) and the Spokane join the Columbia above the Snake, while the John Day joins downstream of the Snake, in the Columbia River Gorge. It is of note that the northeastern divide of the Snake River watershed forms the Idaho-Montana boundary, so the Snake River watershed does not extend into Montana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NorthAmericaWatersheds_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-NorthAmericaWatersheds-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mountain ranges in the Snake watershed include the Teton Range, <a href="/wiki/Bitterroot_Range" title="Bitterroot Range">Bitterroot Range</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clearwater_Mountains" title="Clearwater Mountains">Clearwater Mountains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seven_Devils_Mountains" title="Seven Devils Mountains">Seven Devils Mountains</a>, and the extreme northwestern end of the <a href="/wiki/Wind_River_Range" title="Wind River Range">Wind River Range</a>. <a href="/wiki/Grand_Teton" title="Grand Teton">Grand Teton</a> is the highest point in the Snake River watershed, reaching 13,775 feet (4,199 m) in elevation. The elevation of the Snake River is 358 feet (109 m) when it joins the Columbia River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GNISsnake_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-GNISsnake-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollution">Pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Agricultural runoff from farms and <a href="/wiki/Ranch" title="Ranch">ranches</a> in the Snake River Plain and many other areas has severely hurt the ecology of the river throughout the 20th century. After the first irrigation dams on the river begun operation in the first decade of the 20th century, much of the <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable land</a> in a strip a few miles wide along the Snake River was <a href="/wiki/Tillage" title="Tillage">cultivated</a> or turned to <a href="/wiki/Pasture" title="Pasture">pasture</a>, and agricultural return flows began to pollute the Snake. Runoff from several <a href="/wiki/Feedlot" title="Feedlot">feedlots</a> was dumped into the river until laws made the practice illegal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">Fertilizer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a> and other chemicals and pollutants washed into the river greatly increase the nutrient load, especially of <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fecal_coliform" title="Fecal coliform">fecal coliforms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>. During low water, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algae_bloom" title="Algae bloom">algae blooms</a> occur throughout the calm stretches of the river, depleting its oxygen supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milner-dam-id-us.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="232" data-file-width="349" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Milner-dam-id-us.jpg/220px-Milner-dam-id-us.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Milner-dam-id-us.jpg/330px-Milner-dam-id-us.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/42/Milner-dam-id-us.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Milner-dam-id-us.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dams like <a href="/wiki/Milner_Dam" title="Milner Dam">Milner Dam</a> divert water from the Snake River for irrigation, which causes many of the pollution problems in the Snake</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Much of the return flows do not issue directly back into the Snake River, but rather feed the Snake River Aquifer underneath the Snake River Plain. Water diverted from the river for irrigation, after absorbing any surface pollutants, re-enters the ground and feeds the aquifer. Although the aquifer has maintained its level, it has become increasingly laced with contaminants. Water in the aquifer eventually travels to the west side of the Snake River Plain and re-enters the river as springs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Throughout much of the Snake River Plain and Hells Canyon, excessive sediment is also a recurring problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In December 2007, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="U.S. Environmental Protection Agency">U.S. Environmental Protection Agency</a> (EPA) issued a permit requiring owners of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fish_farm" title="Fish farm">fish farms</a> along the Snake River to reduce their phosphorus discharge by 40%. Pollutant levels in Hells Canyon upstream of the Salmon River confluence, including that of water temperature, dissolved nutrients, and sediment, are required to meet certain levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discharge">Discharge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Discharge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Snake River's average flow is 54,830 cubic feet per second (1,553 m<sup>3</sup>/s). The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a> recorded the river's discharge from a period of 1963–2000 at a <a href="/wiki/Stream_gauge" title="Stream gauge">stream gauge</a> below <a href="/wiki/Ice_Harbor_Dam" title="Ice Harbor Dam">Ice Harbor Dam</a>. In that period, the largest average annual flow recorded was 84,190 cu ft/s (2,384 m<sup>3</sup>/s) in 1997, and the lowest was 27,100 cu ft/s (770 m<sup>3</sup>/s) in 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iceharbordam_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-iceharbordam-7">[7]</a></sup> The lowest recorded daily mean flow was 2,700 cu ft/s (76 m<sup>3</sup>/s) on February 4, 1979. On August 27, 1965, there was temporarily no flow as a result of testing at Ice Harbor Dam. The highest recorded flow was 312,000 cu ft/s (8,800 m<sup>3</sup>/s) on June 19, 1974.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iceharbordam_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-iceharbordam-7">[7]</a></sup> The highest flow ever recorded on the Snake River was at a different USGS stream gauge near Clarkston, which operated from 1915 to 1972. This gauge recorded a maximum flow of 369,000 cu ft/s (10,400 m<sup>3</sup>/s)—more than the Columbia's average discharge—on May 29, 1948. An even larger peak discharge, estimated at 409,000 cu ft/s (11,600 m<sup>3</sup>/s), occurred during the flood of June 1894.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usace_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-usace-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The river's flow is also measured at several other points in its course. Above Jackson Lake, Wyoming, the discharge is about 885 cu ft/s (25.1 m<sup>3</sup>/s) from a drainage area of 486 square miles (1,260 km<sup>2</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Minidoka,_Idaho" title="Minidoka, Idaho">Minidoka, Idaho</a>, about halfway through the Snake River Plain, the river's discharge rises to 7,841 cu ft/s (222.0 m<sup>3</sup>/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> However, at <a href="/wiki/Buhl,_Idaho" title="Buhl, Idaho">Buhl, Idaho</a>, only about 50 miles (80 km) downstream, the river's flow decreases to 4,908 cu ft/s (139.0 m<sup>3</sup>/s) because of agricultural diversions and seepage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> But at the border of Idaho and Oregon, near <a href="/wiki/Weiser,_Idaho" title="Weiser, Idaho">Weiser</a> at the beginning of Hells Canyon, the Snake's flow rises to 17,780 cu ft/s (503 m<sup>3</sup>/s) after receiving several major tributaries such as the Payette, Owyhee and <a href="/wiki/Malheur_River" title="Malheur River">Malheur</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The discharge further increases to 19,530 cu ft/s (553 m<sup>3</sup>/s) at Hells Canyon Dam on the border of Idaho and Oregon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Anatone,_Washington" title="Anatone, Washington">Anatone, Washington</a>, downstream of the confluence with the Salmon, one of the Snake's largest tributaries, the mean discharge is 34,560 cu ft/s (979 m<sup>3</sup>/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Snake_River_Scene_1920.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="257" data-file-width="372" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Snake_River_Scene_1920.jpg/220px-Snake_River_Scene_1920.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Snake_River_Scene_1920.jpg/330px-Snake_River_Scene_1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/01/Snake_River_Scene_1920.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Snake_River_Scene_1920.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Snake River Scene</i> (1920) by L.T. Walter</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Canadian explorer <a href="/wiki/David_Thompson_(explorer)" title="David Thompson (explorer)">David Thompson</a> first recorded the Native American name of the Snake River as <i>Shawpatin</i> when he arrived at its mouth by boat in 1800. When the <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Clark_Expedition" title="Lewis and Clark Expedition">Lewis and Clark Expedition</a> crossed westwards into the Snake River watershed in 1805, they first gave it the name <i>Lewis River</i>, <i>Lewis Fork</i> or <i>Lewis's Fork</i>, as <a href="/wiki/Meriwether_Lewis" title="Meriwether Lewis">Meriwether Lewis</a> was the first of their group to sight the river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> They also made note of the "Snake Indians" who lived along the river, who were actually the <a href="/wiki/Shoshone" title="Shoshone">Shoshone</a> tribe, and learned that the Native Americans called the river <i>Ki-moo-e-nim</i> or <i>Yam-pah-pa</i> (for an herb that grew prolifically along its banks).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Later American explorers, some of whom were originally part of the Lewis and Clark expedition, journeyed into the Snake River watershed and records show a variety of names have been associated with the river. The explorer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wilson_Price_Hunt" title="Wilson Price Hunt">Wilson Price Hunt</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astor_Expedition" title="Astor Expedition">Astor Expedition</a> named the river as <i>Mad River</i>. Others gave the river names including <i>Shoshone River</i> (after the tribe) and <i>Saptin River</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GNISsnake_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-GNISsnake-2">[2]</a></sup> Eventually, the name <i>Snake River</i> was derived from an S-shaped gesture the Shoshone tribe made with their hands to represent swimming salmon. Explorers misinterpreted it to represent a <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snake</a>, giving the river its present-day name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_inhabitants">Early inhabitants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Early inhabitants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Adams_The_Tetons_and_the_Snake_River.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2402" data-file-width="3000" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Adams_The_Tetons_and_the_Snake_River.jpg/300px-Adams_The_Tetons_and_the_Snake_River.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Adams_The_Tetons_and_the_Snake_River.jpg/450px-Adams_The_Tetons_and_the_Snake_River.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Adams_The_Tetons_and_the_Snake_River.jpg/600px-Adams_The_Tetons_and_the_Snake_River.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Adams_The_Tetons_and_the_Snake_River.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The Tetons - Snake River</i> (1942) by <a href="/wiki/Ansel_Adams" title="Ansel Adams">Ansel Adams</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>People have been living along the Snake River for at least 11,000 years. Historian Daniel S. Meatte divides the prehistory of the western Snake River Basin into three main phases or "adaptive systems". The first he calls "Broad Spectrum Foraging", dating from 11,500 to 4,200 years before present. During this period people drew upon a wide variety of food resources. The second period, "Semisedentary Foraging", dates from 4,200–250 years before present and is distinctive for an increased reliance upon fish, especially salmon, as well as food preservation and storage. The third phase, from 250 to 100 years before present, he calls "Equestrian Foragers". It is characterized by large horse-mounted tribes that spent long amounts of time away from their local foraging range hunting bison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> In the eastern Snake River Plain there is some evidence of <a href="/wiki/Clovis_culture" title="Clovis culture">Clovis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Folsom_tradition" title="Folsom tradition">Folsom</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plano_cultures" title="Plano cultures">Plano cultures</a> dating back over 10,000 years ago.</p>
<p>Early fur traders and explorers noted regional trading centers, and archaeological evidence has shown some to be of considerable antiquity. One such trading center in the Weiser area existed as early as 4,500 years ago. The <a href="/wiki/Fremont_culture" title="Fremont culture">Fremont culture</a> may have contributed to the historic Shoshones, but it is not well understood. Another poorly understood early cultural component is called the Midvale Complex. The introduction of the horse to the Snake River Plain around 1700 helped in establishing the Shoshone and Northern <a href="/wiki/Paiute" title="Paiute">Paiute</a> cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fremont_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fremont-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the Snake River in southeastern Washington there are several ancient sites. One of the oldest and most well-known is called the Marmes Rockshelter, which was used from over 11,000 years ago to relatively recent times. The <a href="/wiki/Marmes_Rockshelter" title="Marmes Rockshelter">Marmes Rockshelter</a> was flooded in 1968 by <a href="/wiki/Lake_Herbert_G._West" title="Lake Herbert G. West">Lake Herbert G. West</a>, the Lower Monumental Dam's reservoir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Eventually, two large Native American groups controlled most of the Snake River: the Nez Perce, whose territory stretched from the southeastern Columbia Plateau into northern Oregon and western Idaho, and the Shoshone, who occupied the Snake River Plain both above and below Shoshone Falls. Lifestyles along the Snake River varied widely. Below Shoshone Falls, the economy centered on salmon, who often came up the river in enormous numbers. Salmon were the mainstay of the Nez Perce and most of the other tribes below Shoshone Falls. Above the falls, life was significantly different. The Snake River Plain forms one of the only relatively easy paths across the main Rocky Mountains for many hundreds of miles, allowing Native Americans both east and west of the mountains to interact. As a result, the Shoshone centered on a trading economy.</p>
<p>According to legend, the Nez Perce tribe was first founded in the valley of the Clearwater River, one of the Snake River's lowermost major tributaries. At its height, there were at least 27 Nez Perce settlements along the Clearwater River and 11 more on the Snake between the mouth of the Clearwater and <a href="/wiki/Imnaha_River" title="Imnaha River">Imnaha Rivers</a>. There were also villages on the Salmon River, Grande Ronde River, <a href="/wiki/Tucannon_River" title="Tucannon River">Tucannon River</a>, and the lower Hells Canyon area. The Snake River's annual salmon run, which was estimated at that time to exceed four million in good years, supported the Nez Perce, who lived in permanent, well-defined villages, unlike the nomadic southeastern tribes along the Snake River. The Nez Perce also were involved in trade with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flathead_Indian" title="Flathead Indian">Flathead</a> tribe to the north and other middle Columbia River tribes. However, they were enemies to the Shoshone and the other upstream Snake River tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Shoshone or Shoshoni were characterized by nomadic groups that took their culture from the earlier Bitterroot culture and <a href="/wiki/Great_Basin" title="Great Basin">Great Basin</a> tribes that migrated north via the Owyhee River. They were the most powerful tribe in the Rocky Mountains area, and were known to many <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a> tribes as the "Snakes". In the 18th century, Shoshone territory extended beyond the Snake River Plain, extending over the Continental Divide into the upper Missouri River watershed and even further north into <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> epidemic brought by European explorers and fur trappers was responsible for wiping out much of the Shoshone east of the Rocky Mountains, but the Shoshone continued to occupy the Snake River Plain. Eventually, the Shoshone culture merged with that of the Paiute and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bannock_(tribe)" title="Bannock (tribe)">Bannock</a> tribes, which came from the Great Basin and the Hells Canyon area, respectively. The Bannock brought with them the skill of <a href="/wiki/Bison" title="Bison">buffalo</a> hunting and <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">horses</a> they had acquired from Europeans, changing the Shoshone way of life significantly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration_and_settling">Exploration and settling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Exploration and settling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Lewis and Clark Expedition (1804–06) was the first American group to cross the Rocky Mountains and sail down the Snake and Columbia rivers to the Pacific Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gulick17_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-gulick17-62">[62]</a></sup> Meriwether Lewis supposedly became the first American to sight the drainage basin of the Snake River after he crossed the mountains a few days ahead of his party on August 12, 1805, and sighted the Salmon River valley (a major Snake tributary) from <a href="/wiki/Lemhi_Pass" title="Lemhi Pass">Lemhi Pass</a>, a few miles from the present-day site of <a href="/wiki/Salmon,_Idaho" title="Salmon, Idaho">Salmon, Idaho</a>. The party later traveled north, descended the <a href="/wiki/Lemhi_River" title="Lemhi River">Lemhi River</a> to the Salmon and attempted to descend it to the Snake, but found it impassable because of its violent rapids. The expedition named the Snake River the <i>Lewis River</i>, <i>Lewis's River</i>, or <i>Lewis Fork</i>, in his honor, and they traveled northwards to the <a href="/wiki/Lochsa_River" title="Lochsa River">Lochsa River</a>, which they traveled via the Clearwater River into the lower Snake, and into the Columbia. They also referred to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shoshone_Indians" title="Shoshone Indians">Shoshone Indians</a> as the "Snake Indians", which became the present-day name of the river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The name "Lewis Fork", however, did not last.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gulick17_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-gulick17-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Snake_River_near_Taylor%27s_Bridge,_by_Jackson,_William_Henry,_1843-1942_cleaned.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1363" data-file-width="1115" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Snake_River_near_Taylor%27s_Bridge%2C_by_Jackson%2C_William_Henry%2C_1843-1942_cleaned.jpg/220px-Snake_River_near_Taylor%27s_Bridge%2C_by_Jackson%2C_William_Henry%2C_1843-1942_cleaned.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Snake_River_near_Taylor%27s_Bridge%2C_by_Jackson%2C_William_Henry%2C_1843-1942_cleaned.jpg/330px-Snake_River_near_Taylor%27s_Bridge%2C_by_Jackson%2C_William_Henry%2C_1843-1942_cleaned.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Snake_River_near_Taylor%27s_Bridge%2C_by_Jackson%2C_William_Henry%2C_1843-1942_cleaned.jpg/440px-Snake_River_near_Taylor%27s_Bridge%2C_by_Jackson%2C_William_Henry%2C_1843-1942_cleaned.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Snake_River_near_Taylor%27s_Bridge,_by_Jackson,_William_Henry,_1843-1942_cleaned.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Snake River near Jackson, Wyoming, in 1871</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Later American explorers traveled throughout the Snake River area and up its major tributaries beginning in 1806, just after Lewis and Clark had returned. The first was <a href="/wiki/John_Ordway" title="John Ordway">John Ordway</a> in 1806, who also explored the lower Salmon River. <a href="/wiki/John_Colter" title="John Colter">John Colter</a> in 1808 was the first to sight the upper headwaters of the Snake River, including the Jackson Hole area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-explorers_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-explorers-65">[65]</a></sup> In 1810, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Henry_(fur_trader)" title="Andrew Henry (fur trader)">Andrew Henry</a>, along with a party of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fur_trapper" title="Fur trapper">fur trappers</a>, discovered the Henrys Fork of the Snake River, which is now named after him. <a href="/wiki/Donald_Mackenzie_(explorer)" title="Donald Mackenzie (explorer)">Donald Mackenzie</a> sailed the lower Snake River in 1811, and later explorers included Wilson Price Hunt of the Astor Expedition (who gave the river the name "Mad River"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ramsay_Crooks" title="Ramsay Crooks">Ramsay Crooks</a>, Francisco Payelle, John Grey, Thyery Goddin, and many others after the 1830s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-explorers_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-explorers-65">[65]</a></sup> Many of these later explorers were original members of the Lewis and Clark Expedition who had returned to map and explore the area in greater detail. Even later, American fur trappers scouted the area for beaver streams, but Canadian trappers from the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">British</a> <a href="/wiki/Hudson%27s_Bay_Company" title="Hudson's Bay Company">Hudson's Bay Company</a> were by now a major competitor.</p>
<p>The Hudson's Bay Company first sent fur trappers into the Snake River watershed in 1819. The party of three traveled into the headwaters of the Owyhee River, a major southern tributary of the Snake, but disappeared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EOI_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-EOI-67">[67]</a></sup> Meanwhile, as American fur trappers kept coming to the region, the Hudson's Bay Company ordered the Canadian trappers to kill as many beavers as they could, eventually nearly eradicating the species from the Snake River watershed, under the "rationale [that] if there are no beavers, there will be no reason for the Yanks ([Americans]) to come."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EOI_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-EOI-67">[67]</a></sup> Their goal was to eventually gain rights over the <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Territory" title="Oregon Territory">Oregon Territory</a>, a region covering Washington, Oregon, Idaho, and parts of Montana and Wyoming (most of the present-day region called the Pacific Northwest).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> However, the area was eventually annexed into the United States.</p>
<p>By the middle 19th century, the Oregon Trail had been established, generally following much of the Snake River. One crossing the trail made over the Snake River was near the present-day site of <a href="/wiki/Glenns_Ferry,_Idaho" title="Glenns Ferry, Idaho">Glenns Ferry</a>. Several years later, a ferry was established at the site, replacing the old system where pioneers had to ford the wide, powerful and deep Snake. Another place where pioneers crossed the Snake was further upstream, at a place called "Three Island Crossing", near the mouth of the Boise River. This area has a group of three islands (hence the name) that splits the Snake into four channels each about 200 feet (61 m) wide. Some emigrants chose to ford the Snake and proceed down the west side and recross the river near <a href="/wiki/Fort_Boise" title="Fort Boise">Fort Boise</a> into Hells Canyon, continue down the drier east side into the gorge, or float the Snake and Columbia to the <a href="/wiki/Willamette_River" title="Willamette River">Willamette River</a>, the destination of the Oregon Trail. The reason for the Three Island Crossing was the better availability of grass and water access.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Numerous ferries have provided crossings of the upper Snake from the Brownlee Ferry at the head of Hell's Canyon<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> to <a href="/wiki/Menor%27s_Ferry" title="Menor's Ferry">Menor's Ferry</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> which operates today at Moose, Wyoming. Sophistication varied from <a href="/wiki/Reed_boat" title="Reed boat">reed boats</a> pulled by Indians on horse back at Snake Fort, Fort Boise, as described by <a href="/wiki/Narcissa_Whitman" title="Narcissa Whitman">Narcissa Whitman</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> in 1836 to an electric operated ferry, the Swan Falls Ferry,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> at <a href="/wiki/Swan_Falls_Dam" title="Swan Falls Dam">Swan Falls Dam</a> of the early 20th century.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steamboats">Steamboats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Steamboats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Steamboats_of_the_Columbia_River" title="Steamboats of the Columbia River">Steamboats of the Columbia River</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Steamboat_coming_up_Snake_River_near_Bishop_landing_1898.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="281" data-file-width="350" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Steamboat_coming_up_Snake_River_near_Bishop_landing_1898.JPG/220px-Steamboat_coming_up_Snake_River_near_Bishop_landing_1898.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Steamboat_coming_up_Snake_River_near_Bishop_landing_1898.JPG/330px-Steamboat_coming_up_Snake_River_near_Bishop_landing_1898.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d4/Steamboat_coming_up_Snake_River_near_Bishop_landing_1898.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Steamboat_coming_up_Snake_River_near_Bishop_landing_1898.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An unknown steamboat sails up the Snake River in either Washington or Idaho in 1898</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Unlike the Columbia River, it was far more difficult for steamboats to navigate on the Snake. The Columbia River drops 2,690 feet (820 m) from source to mouth, while the Snake drops over 8,500 feet (2,600 m) in elevation over a length more than 200 miles (320 km) shorter. Still, from the 1860s to the 1940s, steamboats traveled on the Snake River from its mouth at the Columbia River to near the mouth of the Imnaha River in lower Hells Canyon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> However, most of the steamboats only sailed from the river's mouth to Lewiston, located at the confluence of the Snake and Clearwater rivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hlsteamboats_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-hlsteamboats-75">[75]</a></sup> This stretch of the river is the easiest to navigate for watercraft since it has the least elevation change, although it still contained over 60 sets of rapids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Passenger and freight service downstream of Lewiston lasted throughout the late 19th century and persisted until the introduction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Railroad" title="Railroad">railroads</a> in the Palouse Hills <a href="/wiki/Grain" title="Grain">grain</a>-growing region and ultimately, the construction of dams on the lower Snake to facilitate <a href="/wiki/Barge" title="Barge">barge</a> traffic, which caused the demise of both the steamboats and the railroad. Lewiston, 140 miles (230 km) from the confluence of the Snake and Columbia and 465 miles (748 km) from the mouth of the Columbia on the Pacific Ocean, became connected with <a href="/wiki/Portland,_Oregon" title="Portland, Oregon">Portland</a> and other Pacific ports via steamboat service from the mouth of the Snake through the Columbia River Gorge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> A commonly traveled route was from <a href="/wiki/Wallula,_Washington" title="Wallula, Washington">Wallula, Washington</a>, 120 miles (190 km) downstream of the Snake River's mouth, upstream to Lewiston.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Steam_Navigation_Company" title="Oregon Steam Navigation Company">Oregon Steam Navigation Company</a> launched the <a href="/wiki/Shoshone_(Snake_River_sternwheeler)" title="Shoshone (Snake River sternwheeler)">Shoshone</a> at Fort Boise in 1866 which provided passenger and freight service on the upper Snake for the Boise and Owyhee mines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the 1870s, the OSN Company, owned by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Pacific_Railroad" title="Northern Pacific Railroad">Northern Pacific Railroad</a>, was operating seven steamboats for transporting <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a> and grain from the productive Palouse region along the Snake and Columbia to lower Columbia River ports. These boats were the <i>Harvest Queen</i>, <i>John Gates</i>, <i>Spokane</i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annie_Faxon_(sternwheeler)" title="Annie Faxon (sternwheeler)">Annie Faxon</a></i>, <i>Mountain Queen</i>, <i>R.R. Thompson</i>, and <i>Wide West</i>, all of which were built on the Columbia River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> However, there were more resources along the Snake River than wheat and grain. In the 1890s, a huge <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> deposit was discovered at Eureka Bar in Hells Canyon. Several ships were built specifically to transport ore from there to Lewiston: these included <i>Imnaha</i>, <i>Mountain Gem</i>, and <i>Norma</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> In 1893 the <i>Annie Faxon</i> suffered a boiler explosion and sank on the Snake below Lewiston.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hlsteamboats_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-hlsteamboats-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="River_modifications">River modifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: River modifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dams">Dams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Dams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_dams_of_the_Columbia_River_watershed" title="List of dams of the Columbia River watershed">List of dams of the Columbia River watershed</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_crossings_of_the_Snake_River" title="List of crossings of the Snake River">List of crossings of the Snake River</a></div>
<p>Many factors have influenced the construction of dams along the Snake River. A total of fifteen dams have been constructed along the Snake River for a multitude of different purposes, from its headwaters in the Rocky Mountains to its mouth on <a href="/wiki/Lake_Wallula" title="Lake Wallula">Lake Wallula</a>, a slackwater reservoir formed behind McNary Dam on the Columbia River. Dams on the Snake can be grouped into three major categories. From its headwaters to the beginning of Hells Canyon, many small dams block the Snake to provide <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> water. Between here and Hells Canyon, the first dam on the Snake, Swan Falls Dam, was built in 1901. In Hells Canyon, a cascade of dams produce <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a> from the river's lofty decrease in elevation over a comparatively small distance. Finally, a third cascade of dams, from Hells Canyon to the mouth, facilitates navigation. Many different government and private agencies have worked to build dams on the Snake River, which now serve an important purpose for people living in the Snake's drainage basin and trade of agricultural products to Pacific seaports.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minidoka_Irrigation_Project" title="Minidoka Irrigation Project">Minidoka Irrigation Project</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Bureau_of_Reclamation" title="U.S. Bureau of Reclamation">U.S. Bureau of Reclamation</a>, created with the passage of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reclamation_Act" title="Reclamation Act">Reclamation Act</a> of 1902, involved the diversion of Snake River water into the Snake River Plain upstream of Shoshone Falls in order to irrigate approximately 1,100,000 acres (4,500 km<sup>2</sup>) in the Snake River Plain and store 4,100,000 acre feet (5.1 km<sup>3</sup>) of water in Snake River reservoirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The first studies for irrigation in the Plain were conducted by the United States Geological Survey in the late 19th century, and the project was authorized on April 23, 1904.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MinidokaProject_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-MinidokaProject-84">[84]</a></sup> The first dam constructed for the project was Minidoka Dam in 1904; its power plant began operating in 1909, producing 7 MW of electricity. This capacity was revised to 20 MW in 1993.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> However, Minidoka Dam was not the only dam constructed for the project. As far upstream as Jackson Lake in Wyoming, the <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Lake_Dam" title="Jackson Lake Dam">Jackson Lake Dam</a> was built in 1907 to raise the lake level for providing additional water storage for dry years. American Falls Dam, upstream of Minidoka, was completed in 1927 and replaced in 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MinidokaProject_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-MinidokaProject-84">[84]</a></sup> As the dams were constructed above Shoshone Falls, the historical upriver limit of salmon and also a total barrier to boats and ships, no provisions were made for fish passage or navigation. Several other irrigation dams were also built - including <a href="/wiki/Twin_Falls_(Idaho)" title="Twin Falls (Idaho)">Twin Falls Dam</a> and Palisades Dam.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brownlee.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="267" data-file-width="400" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Brownlee.jpg/220px-Brownlee.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Brownlee.jpg/330px-Brownlee.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c4/Brownlee.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brownlee.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Brownlee_Dam" title="Brownlee Dam">Brownlee Dam</a>, the uppermost dam of the Hells Canyon Project</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Hells Canyon Project was built and maintained by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Idaho_Power_Company" title="Idaho Power Company">Idaho Power Company</a> starting in the 1940s, and was the second of the three major water projects on the river. The three dams of the project, Brownlee Dam, Oxbow Dam and Hells Canyon Dam, are located in upper Hells Canyon. All three dams are primarily for power generation and flood control, and do not have fish passage or navigation locks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HellsCanyonProject_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-HellsCanyonProject-86">[86]</a></sup> Brownlee Dam, the most upriver dam, was constructed in 1959, and generates 728 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt" title="Megawatt">MW</a> of power. Oxbow Dam, the second dam in the project, was built in 1961 and generates 220 MW. The dam was named for a 3-mile-wide (4.8 km) bend in the Snake River, shaped like an oxbow, although not an <a href="/wiki/Oxbow_lake" title="Oxbow lake">oxbow lake</a>. Hells Canyon Dam was the last and most downriver of the three, was constructed in 1967 and generates 450 MW.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IdahoPowerDams_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-IdahoPowerDams-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
