<p>Downriver of the Hells Canyon is the Lower Snake River Project, authorized by the <a href="/wiki/Rivers_and_Harbors_Act#List_of_later_acts" title="Rivers and Harbors Act">Rivers and Harbors Act of 1945</a>, which was created by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army_Corps_of_Engineers" title="U.S. Army Corps of Engineers">U.S. Army Corps of Engineers</a> to create a navigable channel on the Snake River from its mouth to the beginning of Hells Canyon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LowerNavigation_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-LowerNavigation-87">[87]</a></sup> These dams are, in downstream order: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_Granite_Lock_and_Dam" title="Lower Granite Lock and Dam">Lower Granite Lock and Dam</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Little_Goose_Lock_and_Dam" title="Little Goose Lock and Dam">Little Goose Lock and Dam</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_Monumental_Lock_and_Dam" title="Lower Monumental Lock and Dam">Lower Monumental Lock and Dam</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_Harbor_Lock_and_Dam" title="Ice Harbor Lock and Dam">Ice Harbor Lock and Dam</a>. <a href="/wiki/Dredging" title="Dredging">Dredging</a> work was also done throughout the length of the navigation channel to facilitate ship passage. These dams form a cascade of reservoirs with no stretches of free-flowing river in between. Immediately below Ice Harbor Dam is Lake Wallula, formed by the construction of the McNary Dam on the Columbia River. (McNary Dam is not part of the Lower Snake River Project.) Above Lower Granite Dam, the river channel from Lewiston to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Johnson_Bar,_Idaho&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Johnson Bar, Idaho (page does not exist)">Johnson Bar</a>, just below Hells Canyon, is also maintained for jet-boats as this section is too rugged for ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> These dams have been proposed for removal, and if they were to be removed, it would be the largest <a href="/wiki/Dam_removal" title="Dam removal">dam removal</a> project ever undertaken in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The removal has been proposed on the grounds that it would restore salmon runs to the lower Snake River and the Clearwater River and other smaller tributaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Idaho's Snake river once teemed with sockeye salmon. However, there are almost no wild sockeye salmon left in the river due to a number of factors.</p>
<p>There are many reasons why Sockeye Salmon in the Snake River are reduced in number. One reason is that the river runs through three different states, and is over 1,000 miles (1,600 km) long. Salmon swimming upstream in this river are faced with predators and dams. The Snake River has fifteen dams and is extremely difficult for salmon to access because of hydroelectric dams. Hell's Canyon Dam blocks passage to the entire upper Snake River. The <a href="/wiki/Grand_Coulee_Dam" title="Grand Coulee Dam">Grand Coulee Dam</a> also blocks spawning grounds to the famous "June Hogs" (legendary <a href="/wiki/Chinook_salmon" title="Chinook salmon">Chinook salmon</a> that weighed over 100 pounds [45 kg]).</p>
<p>Between 1985 and 2007, only an average of 18 sockeye salmon returned to Idaho each year. Serious conservation efforts by wildlife biologists and fish hatcheries have captured the few remaining wild sockeye salmon, collected their sperm and eggs, and in a laboratory, have them spawn. Instead of spawning naturally, these sockeye begin their lives in an incubator in a fishery biologist's laboratory. These baby salmon then are transported by ship, bypassing the dams. (The dams can hurt juvenile baby sockeye salmon with their powerful tides and currents, which suck the baby salmon down.) Another conservation effort that has helped the salmon recover, is the destruction of old, outdated dams, such as the Lewiston Dam on the Clearwater River, a tributary of the Snake. After destroying the dam, salmon populations noticeably recovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another interesting recovery method conservationists and biologists are using is called Fish Transportation. Since many juvenile salmon perish at each dam while swimming out to the ocean, massive ships filter and collect these baby salmon by size and take them out to the ocean for a ride, where they can be guaranteed to make it alive to saltwater. This method raises controversy to the effectiveness and costs, since this method is extremely expensive, almost costing $15 million. Another similar method to transport fish across the dams is the "Fish Gun" method. Engineers at Whooshh Innovations have developed a "Whooshh Fish Transport System" that literally collects salmon and shoots them above the dam at high speed in an effort to get them across the dams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Overall, these combined efforts have had good success. In the summer of 2006, the Snake River reportedly only had 3 sockeye salmon that returned to their spawning grounds.In the summer of 2013, more than 13,000 sockeye salmon returned to the spawning grounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LGLandDam2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="310" data-file-width="466" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/LGLandDam2.jpg/220px-LGLandDam2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/LGLandDam2.jpg/330px-LGLandDam2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/LGLandDam2.jpg/440px-LGLandDam2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LGLandDam2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Little_Goose_Lock_and_Dam" title="Little Goose Lock and Dam">Little Goose Lock and Dam</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Navigation">Navigation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Navigation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 1960s and 1970s the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers built four dams and locks on the lower Snake River to facilitate shipping. The lower Columbia River has likewise been dammed for navigation. Thus a deep <a href="/wiki/Channel_(geography)" title="Channel (geography)">shipping channel</a> through locks and slackwater reservoirs for heavy barges exists from the Pacific Ocean to Lewiston, Idaho. Most barge traffic originating on the Snake River goes to deep-water ports on the lower Columbia River, such as Portland. Grain, mostly wheat, is the main product shipped from the Snake, and nearly all of it is exported internationally from the lower Columbia River ports.</p>
<p>The shipping channel is authorized to be at least 14 feet (4 m) deep and 250 feet (76 m) wide. Where river depths were less than 14 feet (4 m), the shipping channel has been dredged in most places. Dredging and redredging work is ongoing and actual depths vary over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lsr_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-lsr-94">[94]</a></sup> With a channel about 5 feet (1.5 m) deeper than the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> system, the Columbia and Snake rivers can float barges twice as heavy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Agricultural products from Idaho and eastern Washington are among the main goods transported by barge on the Snake and Columbia rivers. Grain, mainly wheat, accounts for more than 85% of the cargo barged on the lower Snake River. In 1998, over 123,000,000 US bushels (4.3<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> l; 980,000,000 US dry gal; 950,000,000 imp gal) of grain were barged on the Snake. Before the completion of the lower Snake dams, grain from the region was transported by truck or rail to Columbia River ports around the Tri-Cities. Other products barged on the lower Snake River include peas, lentils, forest products, and petroleum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lsr_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-lsr-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology">Biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pacific_Northwest_River_System.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2032" data-file-width="1930" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Pacific_Northwest_River_System.png/200px-Pacific_Northwest_River_System.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Pacific_Northwest_River_System.png/300px-Pacific_Northwest_River_System.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Pacific_Northwest_River_System.png/400px-Pacific_Northwest_River_System.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pacific_Northwest_River_System.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Columbia River Basin</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Fund_for_Nature" title="World Wide Fund for Nature">World Wide Fund for Nature</a> (WWF) divides the Snake River's watershed into two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater_ecoregion" title="Freshwater ecoregion">freshwater ecoregions</a>: the "Columbia Unglaciated" ecoregion and the "Upper Snake" ecoregion. Shoshone Falls marks the boundary between the two. The WWF placed the ecoregion boundary about 50 kilometres (31 mi) downriver from Shoshone Falls in order to include the <a href="/wiki/Big_Wood_River" title="Big Wood River">Big Wood River</a> (the main tributary of the <a href="/wiki/Malad_River" title="Malad River">Malad River</a>) in the Upper Snake ecoregion, because the Wood River is biologically distinct from the rest of the downriver Snake. Shoshone Falls has presented a total barrier to the upstream movement of fish for 30,000 to 60,000 years. As a result, only 35% of the fish fauna above the falls, and 40% of the Wood River's fish fauna, are shared with the lower Snake River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Upper Snake freshwater ecoregion includes most of southeastern Idaho and extends into small portions of Wyoming, Utah, and Nevada, including major freshwater habitats such as Jackson Lake. Compared to the lower Snake River and the rest of the Columbia River's watershed, the Upper Snake ecoregion has a high level of <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemism</a>, especially among <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_mollusc" title="Freshwater mollusc">freshwater molluscs</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Gastropoda" title="Gastropoda">snails</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bivalvia" title="Bivalvia">clams</a>. There are at least 21 snail and clam species of special concern, including 15 that appear to exist only in single clusters. There are 14 fish species found in the Upper Snake region that do not occur elsewhere in the Columbia's watershed, but which do occur in Bonneville freshwater ecoregion of western Utah, part of the Great Basin and related to the prehistoric Lake Bonneville. The <a href="/wiki/Wood_River_sculpin" title="Wood River sculpin">Wood River sculpin</a> (<i>Cottus leiopomus</i>) is endemic to the Wood River. The <a href="/wiki/Shoshone_sculpin" title="Shoshone sculpin">Shoshone sculpin</a> (<i>Cottus greenei</i>) is endemic to the small portion of the Snake River between Shoshone Falls and the Wood River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abell_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-abell-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Snake River below Shoshone Falls is home to thirty-five native fish species, of which twelve are also found in the Columbia River and four of which are endemic to the Snake: the relict sand roller (<i><a href="/wiki/Percopsis_transmontana" title="Percopsis transmontana">Percopsis transmontana</a></i>) of the <a href="/wiki/Percopsidae" title="Percopsidae">Percopsidae</a> family, the shorthead <a href="/wiki/Sculpin" title="Sculpin">sculpin</a> (<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cottus_confusus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cottus confusus (page does not exist)">Cottus confusus</a></i>), the maginated sculpin (<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cottus_marginatus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cottus marginatus (page does not exist)">Cottus marginatus</a></i>), and the <a href="/wiki/Oregonichthys" title="Oregonichthys">Oregon chub</a> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oregonichthys_crameri" title="Oregonichthys crameri">Oregonichthys crameri</a></i>). The Oregon chub is also found in the <a href="/wiki/Umpqua_River" title="Umpqua River">Umpqua River</a> and nearby basins. The lower Snake River also supports seven species of Pacific salmon and trout (<i><a href="/wiki/Oncorhynchus" title="Oncorhynchus">Oncorhynchus</a></i>). There are also high, often localized levels of mollusc endemism, especially in Hells Canyon and the basins of the Clearwater River, Salmon River, and middle Snake River. The mollusc richness extends into the lower Columbia River and tributaries such as the <a href="/wiki/Deschutes_River_(Oregon)" title="Deschutes River (Oregon)">Deschutes River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abell_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-abell-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animals">Animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aside from aquatic species, much of the Snake River watershed supports larger animals including numerous species of mammals, birds, amphibians, and reptiles. Especially in the headwaters and the other mountainous areas strewn throughout the watershed, the <a href="/wiki/Gray_wolf" title="Gray wolf">gray wolf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grizzly_bear" title="Grizzly bear">grizzly bear</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wolverine" title="Wolverine">wolverine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mountain_lion" title="Mountain lion">mountain lion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canada_lynx" title="Canada lynx">Canada lynx</a> are common. It has been determined that there are 97 species of mammals in the upper part of the Snake River, upstream from the Henrys Fork confluence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uppersnakeprovince_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-uppersnakeprovince-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pronghorn" title="Pronghorn">Pronghorn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bighorn_sheep" title="Bighorn sheep">bighorn sheep</a> are common in the area drained by the "lost streams of Idaho", several rivers and large creeks that flow south from the Rocky Mountains and disappear into the Snake River Aquifer. About 274 bird species, some endangered or threatened, use the Snake River watershed, including <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whooping_crane" title="Whooping crane">whooping crane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greater_sage-grouse" title="Greater sage-grouse">greater sage-grouse</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yellow-billed_cuckoo" title="Yellow-billed cuckoo">yellow-billed cuckoo</a>. <a href="/wiki/Barrow%27s_goldeneye" title="Barrow's goldeneye">Barrow's goldeneye</a> are a species of bird that occurs commonly along the lower section of the Snake River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uppersnakeprovince_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-uppersnakeprovince-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Snakeriver.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Snakeriver.jpg/220px-Snakeriver.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Snakeriver.jpg/330px-Snakeriver.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Snakeriver.jpg/440px-Snakeriver.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Snakeriver.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In the middle reaches of the Snake River as it flows through the Snake River Plain, introduced species have fared better than native species</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Ten amphibian and twenty species of reptiles inhabit the upper Snake River's <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Riparian_zone" title="Riparian zone">riparian zones</a>. Several species of frogs are common in the "lost streams" basin and the northeasternmost part of the Snake River watershed, including the <a href="/wiki/Tailed_frog" title="Tailed frog">inland tailed frog</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_leopard_frog" title="Northern leopard frog">northern leopard frog</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_toad" title="Western toad">western toad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_spotted_frog" title="Columbia spotted frog">Columbia spotted frog</a>, <a href="/wiki/Long-toed_salamander" title="Long-toed salamander">long-toed salamander</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Spadefoot_Toads" title="American Spadefoot Toads">spadefoot toad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uppersnakeprovince_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-uppersnakeprovince-11">[11]</a></sup> However, in the lower and middle portions of the Snake River watershed, several native species have been severely impacted by agriculture practices and the resulting non-native species supported by them. Introduced birds include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gray_partridge" title="Gray partridge">gray partridge</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ring-necked_pheasant" title="Ring-necked pheasant">ring-necked pheasant</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chukar_partridge" title="Chukar partridge">chukar</a>. Other non-native species include the <a href="/wiki/American_bullfrog" title="American bullfrog">bullfrog</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brown-headed_cowbird" title="Brown-headed cowbird">brown-headed cowbird</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_starling" title="European starling">European starling</a>, attracted by the construction of cities and towns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lowersnakewildlife_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-lowersnakewildlife-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plants">Plants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Plants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oxbow_Bend_overlook_of_Snake_River.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1425" data-file-width="3264" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Oxbow_Bend_overlook_of_Snake_River.JPG/200px-Oxbow_Bend_overlook_of_Snake_River.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Oxbow_Bend_overlook_of_Snake_River.JPG/300px-Oxbow_Bend_overlook_of_Snake_River.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Oxbow_Bend_overlook_of_Snake_River.JPG/400px-Oxbow_Bend_overlook_of_Snake_River.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oxbow_Bend_overlook_of_Snake_River.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The headwaters of the Snake River remain heavily forested, especially inside protected areas</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Snake River watershed includes a diversity of vegetation zones both past and present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uppersnakeprovince_11-6"><a href="#cite_note-uppersnakeprovince-11">[11]</a></sup> A majority of the watershed was once covered with <a href="/wiki/Shrub-steppe" title="Shrub-steppe">shrub-steppe</a> <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grassland</a>, most common in the Snake River Plain and also the Columbia Plateau in southeastern Washington. Riparian zones, wetlands and marshes once occurred along the length of the Snake River and its tributaries. In higher elevations, conifer forests, of which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ponderosa_pine" title="Ponderosa pine">ponderosa pine</a> is most common, dominate the landscape. The basin ranges from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-desert" title="Semi-desert">semi-desert</a> to alpine climates, providing habitat for hundreds of species of plants. In the lowermost part of the watershed, in southeastern Washington, the Snake River is surrounded by an area called the Columbia Plateau Ecoprovince, which is now mostly occupied by irrigated farms. The rest of the Plateau area is characterized by low hills, dry lakes, and an arid, nearly desert climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lowersnakewildlife_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-lowersnakewildlife-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>The headwaters of the Snake River and the high mountains elsewhere in the watershed were historically heavily forested. These include <a href="/wiki/Aspen" title="Aspen">aspen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Douglas_fir" title="Douglas fir">Douglas fir</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spruce_fir" title="Spruce fir">spruce fir</a>, comprising about 20% of the historic watershed. At the base of mountains and in the Lost River basin, <a href="/wiki/Sagebrush" title="Sagebrush">sagebrush</a> was and is the predominant vegetation cover. Because of deforestation, up to one quarter of the forests have been taken over by sagebrush, leaving the remaining forests to cover about 15% of the watershed. However, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lodgepole_pine" title="Lodgepole pine">lodgepole pine</a> has increased in number, taking over historic stands of other conifers. There are also up to 118 species of rare or endemic plants that occur in the Snake River watershed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uppersnakeprovince_11-7"><a href="#cite_note-uppersnakeprovince-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salmon_and_other_anadromous_fish">Salmon and other anadromous fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Salmon and other anadromous fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Snake River was once one of the most important rivers for the spawning of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anadromous_fish" title="Anadromous fish">anadromous fish</a>—which are hatched in the headwaters of rivers, live in the ocean for most of their lives, and return to the river to spawn—in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-salmon_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-salmon-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The river supported species including <a href="/wiki/Chinook_salmon" title="Chinook salmon">chinook salmon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coho_salmon" title="Coho salmon">coho salmon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sockeye_salmon" title="Sockeye salmon">sockeye salmon</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_trout" title="Rainbow trout">steelhead</a>, <a href="/wiki/White_sturgeon" title="White sturgeon">white sturgeon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pacific_lamprey" title="Pacific lamprey">Pacific lamprey</a>. It is known that before the construction of dams on the river, there were three major chinook salmon runs in the Snake River; in the spring, summer and fall, totaling about 120,000 fish, and the sockeye salmon run was about 150,000. The historical barrier to fish migration on the Snake River was Shoshone Falls, a waterfall that occurs as the Snake River passes through the Snake River Plain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-salmon_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-salmon-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hells_Canyon_Oregon.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2344" data-file-width="3563" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Hells_Canyon_Oregon.JPG/220px-Hells_Canyon_Oregon.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Hells_Canyon_Oregon.JPG/330px-Hells_Canyon_Oregon.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Hells_Canyon_Oregon.JPG/440px-Hells_Canyon_Oregon.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hells_Canyon_Oregon.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Reaches such as this one in <a href="/wiki/Hells_Canyon" title="Hells Canyon">Hells Canyon</a> are no longer accessible to salmon due to the construction of dams</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since the early 20th century, when Swan Falls Dam was constructed on the middle Snake River upstream of Hells Canyon, the fifteen dams and reservoirs on the river have posed an increasing problem for migrating salmon. Agricultural lands and their resulting runoff have also had a significant impact on the success rate of migrating fish. Salmon can travel up the Snake River as far as Hells Canyon Dam, using the fish passage facilities of the four lower Snake River dams, leaving the Clearwater, Grande Ronde and Salmon river to sustain spawning salmon. Rising in several forks in the Clearwater Mountains of central Idaho, the Clearwater and Salmon River watersheds are nearly undeveloped with the enormous exception of <a href="/wiki/Dworshak_Dam" title="Dworshak Dam">Dworshak Dam</a> on the <a href="/wiki/North_Fork_Clearwater_River" title="North Fork Clearwater River">North Fork Clearwater River</a>. The watershed of the Grande Ronde in northeastern Oregon is also largely undeveloped. The four reservoirs formed by the lower Snake River dams—<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Sacajawea_(Washington)" title="Lake Sacajawea (Washington)">Lake Sacagawea</a>, Lake Herbert G. West, Lake Bryan, and Lower Granite Lake—have also formed problems, as the downstream current in the pools is often not enough for the fish to sense, confusing their migration routes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HellsCanyonDamProjects_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-HellsCanyonDamProjects-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ClearwaterSalmon_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-ClearwaterSalmon-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the confluence of the Snake and Clearwater Rivers, young salmon that swim down from spawning gravels in the headwaters of the Clearwater River often delay their migrations because of a significant temperature difference. (Prior to the removal of <a href="/wiki/Dam_removal#Completed_dam_removal_projects" title="Dam removal">Lewiston Dam</a> on the main Clearwater and <a href="/wiki/South_Fork_Clearwater_River" title="South Fork Clearwater River">Grangeville Dam</a> on the <a href="/wiki/South_Fork_Clearwater_River" title="South Fork Clearwater River">South Fork Clearwater</a>, the Clearwater was completely unusable by migrating salmon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>) Agricultural runoff and water held in reservoirs higher upstream on the Snake warm its waters as it flows through the Snake River Plain, so as the Snake meets the Clearwater, its average temperature is much higher. Directly below the confluence, the river flows into Lower Granite Lake, formed by <a href="/wiki/Lower_Granite_Dam" title="Lower Granite Dam">Lower Granite Dam</a>, the uppermost dam of the Lower Snake River Project. Paradoxically, the combination of these factors gives the young salmon further time to grow and to feed in Lower Granite Lake, so when they begin the migration to the Pacific Ocean, they often have a higher chance at survival, compared to those salmon who migrate to the ocean earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ClearwaterSalmon_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-ClearwaterSalmon-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lower_Snake_River_dam_removal">Lower Snake River dam removal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Lower Snake River dam removal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A controversy has erupted since the late 20th century over the four lower Snake River dams, with the primary argument being that removing the dams would allow anadromous fish to reach the lower Snake River tributaries—the Clearwater River, the Tucannon River and the Grande Ronde River—and spawn in much higher numbers. However, removal of the dams has been fiercely opposed by some groups in the Pacific Northwest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Because much of the electricity in the Northwest comes from dams, removing the four dams would create a hole in the energy grid that would not be immediately replaceable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Navigation on the lower Snake would also suffer, as submerged riffles, rapids and islands would be exposed by the removal of the dams. Irrigation pumps for fields in southeastern Washington would also have to reach further to access the water of the Snake River. However, aside from restoring salmon runs, <a href="/wiki/Dam_removal" title="Dam removal">dam removal</a> proponents argue that the power is replaceable, that the grain transportation system could be replaced by railroads, and that only one of the four reservoirs supplies irrigation water. Irrigators in the Snake River Plain would likely need to allow less water into the Snake River during low flow in order to create a current in the four lower reservoirs, and recreation and tourism would likely benefit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tributaries">Tributaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Snake_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Tributaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_tributaries_of_the_Columbia_River" title="List of tributaries of the Columbia River">List of tributaries of the Columbia River</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:03-37-14.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1417" data-file-width="2161" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/03-37-14.jpg/220px-03-37-14.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/03-37-14.jpg/330px-03-37-14.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/03-37-14.jpg/440px-03-37-14.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:03-37-14.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Clearwater_River_(Idaho)" title="Clearwater River (Idaho)">Clearwater River</a> is the largest tributary of the Snake River</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Salmon River is the second largest tributary. Although the Salmon has a larger drainage than the Clearwater, the Salmon drains much drier country and therefore has a smaller discharger than the Clearwater, about 8,000,000 acre feet (9.9 km<sup>3</sup>) annually compared to about 11,000,000 acre feet (14 km<sup>3</sup>) annually for the Clearwater River.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Us-id-henrys-fork.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="374" data-file-width="497" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Us-id-henrys-fork.jpg/220px-Us-id-henrys-fork.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Us-id-henrys-fork.jpg/330px-Us-id-henrys-fork.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Us-id-henrys-fork.jpg/440px-Us-id-henrys-fork.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Us-id-henrys-fork.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Henrys_Fork_(Snake_River)" title="Henrys Fork (Snake River)">Henrys Fork</a> is the largest tributary upstream of the Snake River Plain</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Grande_Ronde_River.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="342" data-file-width="471" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Grande_Ronde_River.jpg/220px-Grande_Ronde_River.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Grande_Ronde_River.jpg/330px-Grande_Ronde_River.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Grande_Ronde_River.jpg/440px-Grande_Ronde_River.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Grande_Ronde_River.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Grande_Ronde_River" title="Grande Ronde River">Grande Ronde River</a> meets the Snake near the middle part of Hells Canyon</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Salmon_River.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Salmon_River.JPG/220px-Salmon_River.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Salmon_River.JPG/330px-Salmon_River.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Salmon_River.JPG/440px-Salmon_River.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Salmon_River.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Salmon_River_(Idaho)" title="Salmon River (Idaho)">Salmon River</a> is the second largest tributary, joining the Snake in Hells Canyon</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Snake River has over 20 major <a href="/wiki/Tributary" title="Tributary">tributaries</a>, most of which are in the mountainous regions of the basin. The largest by far is the Clearwater River, which drains 9,000 square miles (23,000 km<sup>2</sup>) in north central Idaho. Many of the rivers that flow into the Snake River Plain from the north sink into the Snake River Aquifer, but still contribute their water to the river. Aside from rivers, the Snake is fed by many significant springs, many of which arise from the aquifer on the west side of the plain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uppersnakeprovince_11-8"><a href="#cite_note-uppersnakeprovince-11">[11]</a></sup></p>

<td>503 sq mi (1,300 km<sup>2</sup>)</td>
<td>Left</td>

<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The percentage is calculated by adding the discharge at <a href="/wiki/Priest_Rapids_Dam" title="Priest Rapids Dam">Priest Rapids Dam</a> on the Columbia to the discharge at <a href="/wiki/Ice_Harbor_Dam" title="Ice Harbor Dam">Ice Harbor Dam</a> on the Snake.
